Title: Higher-Order Debiased Estimators for General Treatment Models

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.01706v1

Published Time: Tue, 02 Jun 2026 02:41:16 GMT

Number of Pages: 90

Markdown Content:
# Higher-Order Debiased Estimators for General Treatment Models ∗

Yulin Zhang †1, Lin Liu ‡2, and Zheng Zhang §1 1Institute of Statistics & Big Data, Renmin University of China 

> 2

Institute of Natural Sciences, MOE–LSC, School of Mathematical Sciences, CMA–Shanghai, SJTU–Yale Joint Center for Biostatistics and Data Science, Shanghai Jiao Tong University 

Abstract 

We have witnessed tremendous progress in developing the foundation for econometrics and causal inference in the past decades. The most popular paradigm in the current literature is the classical (first-order) semiparametric theory, in which a key building block is the (first-order) influence function. However, it is now well known that estimators based on influence functions can be sub-optimal in terms of convergence rates in various settings. To address this issue, higher-order influence functions (HOIF) are developed, generalizing the classical semiparametric theory. However, most existing results in this regard focus on treatment effect parameters defined in explicit forms, such as average treatment effects (ATE). In applications, economists are often confronted with tasks of inferring more complex parameters, such as quantile treatment effects (QTE) or effects of complicated treatment regimes/policy. These more complex parameters can often only be implicitly defined as the solution to nonlinear estimating equations, which correspond to M /Z-estimation problems. Our current understanding of these problems is mainly limited to the classical semiparametric theory. Given the foundational role of HOIF for estimating explicit parameters such as ATE, a modest step toward enriching the statistical foundation of econometrics and causal inference is to develop the corresponding higher-order estimators for those more complex parameters. To this end, we consider parameters of a class of non-separable structural models in the econometrics literature and develop a class of higher-order estimators for the target parameters. Statistical properties of these higher-order estimators are derived using recent advances in U -processes theory. Our proposed higher-order estimators relax complexity-reducing assumptions, quantified by Hölder smoothness, imposed on the nuisance parameters compared to existing alternative estimators for many important parameters in this class, including QTE and quantile dose-response functions, among others. Numerical experiments, including simulation studies and a real data analysis, are also conducted to corroborate our theoretical claims and illustrate how higher-order estimators can be used in practice. 

Keywords: Bias Reduction, Causal Inference, General Treatment Models, Higher-Order Influence Functions, Quantile Treatment Effects, Two-Step Procedures

# 1 Introduction 

1.1 Motivation 

Recent decades have witnessed surged interests in the econometrics and causal inference literature on the construction of nearly rate-optimal estimators for parameters/estimands/(statistical) functionals, such as the average treatment effect (ATE). One particular fruitful econometric paradigm is through the lens of semiparametric theory (Schick, 1986; Newey, 1990), which delivers, among other things, the celebrated doubly-robust Augmented Inverse Probability Weighting (AIPW) estimator of the ATE (Robins et al., 1994; Hahn, 1998; Chernozhukov et al., 2018), based on the (first-order) influence function of the target parameter. The asymptotic variance of such estimators is known to achieve the semiparametric efficiency bound , provided that sufficient complexity-reducing assumptions are imposed on the nuisance parameters (the outcome regression and the propensity score in the case of ATE). 

Sub-optimality of first-order estimators 

Despite the popularity of estimators based on influence functions (henceforth, first-order estimators), growing evidence suggests that they can be sub-optimal in certain settings. Specifically, even when the parameter of interest is estimable at the parametric n−1/2 rate, first-order estimators may fail to achieve n−1/2-consistency, letting alone attain the semiparametric efficiency bound. This sub-optimality can arise in a variety of contexts, including, e.g., when the nuisance parameters are assumed to belong to Hölder -type smoothness classes (Liu et al., 2017; Robins et al., 2023), structure-agnostic classes for certain types of parameters (Robins and Ritov, 1997; Balakrishnan et al., 2026; Liu et al., 2024; Jin and Syrgkanis, 2025), or hybrid smoothness–structure-agnostic classes (Bonvini et al., 2024); or when the nuisance parameters are in the many-covariate or small bandwidth settings, where a direct plug-in estimator based on first-order influence functions possibly may lead to a “leave-in” bias (Cattaneo and Jansson, 2018; Cattaneo et al., 2019). Using ATE and classical nonparametric nuisance models as an illustration, when both the outcome regression and the propensity score belong to Hölder smoothness classes with smoothness index s and variable dimension d, standard 1 first-order estimators of ATE are n−1/2-consistent when 2s/d  

> 1+2 s/d

> 0.5, i.e., s/d > 0.5. However, it is well known that s/d > 0.25 is both sufficient and necessary (hence minimal) for ATE to be n−1/2-estimable (Robins et al., 2009, 2023). Indeed, estimators based on higher-order influence functions (HOIFs) (Robins et al., 2008, 2016; Liu et al., 2017), a generalization of the classical first-order influence functions, have been shown to be n−1/2-consistent for ATE when s/d > 0.25 (Robins et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2017). In the remainder of this paper, we refer to these estimators as the “higher-order estimators” for convenience. More importantly, to the best of our knowledge, no other simpler estimators are n−1/2-consistent under this minimal Hölder smoothness condition, despite years of research efforts. These results also prompt recent advances in applying the HOIF framework to statistical problems similar to but more complex than ATE, such as conditional average treatment effects (Kennedy et al., 2024) and average dose-response functions (Colangelo and Lee, 2026; Bonvini and Kennedy, 2022). Our approach is also connected to the literature addressing “leave-in bias” in the many-covariate and small-bandwidth settings (Cattaneo and Jansson, 2018; Cattaneo et al., 2019); see Section 1.2, 

> 1When we add the qualification “standard”, we mean first-order estimators where the nuisance parameter estimates are computed from an independent nuisance sample as in Chernozhukov et al. (2018) or Kallus et al. (2024).

2Remark 8 and Supplementary Material Section S5 for an extended discussion. The present paper extends this line of work to a broader class of parameters central to modern econometric analysis, which will be made precise next. 

Challenges due to implicitly-defined parameters 

The existing HOIF framework primarily concerns parameters that are explicitly defined, including but not limited to ATE and parameters used in conditional independence tests (Shah and Peters, 2020) as prominent examples. However, many causal parameters of interest in modern economics applications lack closed-form expressions and are instead defined as solutions to moment conditions or optimization problems. The quantile treatment effect (QTE) provides a canonical example. In policy evaluation, when outcomes are heterogeneous or heavy-tailed, QTE offers a more informative summary of treatment effects than the mean (Manski, 2004; Firpo, 2007; Chernozhukov and Hansen, 2005; Powell, 2020). Yet, QTE can only be characterized as the solution to a variational problem (M -estimation) or to a moment equation ( Z-estimation) (see Example 2 later). Another prominent example in the recent econometrics literature is the α-expected shortfall (ES) (Fan et al., 2025), which measures the average outcome in the worst α fraction of the distribution. This quantity is of particular interest in risk management and policy evaluation under tail risk, where policymakers are concerned with extreme rather than average outcomes. Borrowing a terminology from Robins et al. (2016), such parameters are implicitly defined . These implicitly defined parameters can pose significant challenges to estimation and inference. Another important challenge arises from the non-separability between the nuisance parameter and the parameter of interest (Su et al., 2019; Chernozhukov et al., 2025). Using QTE with binary treatment as an example, as shown in Example 2 and Remark 2 later, one of the nuisance parameters, the generalized outcome regression model, depends on the true but unknown QTE parameter. To address this problem, Kallus et al. (2024) develop the localized debiased machine learning (LDML) estimator, which first produces an initial estimator of the QTE using only the propensity score independent of the QTE itself. With this initial estimator, LDML proceeds to estimate the generalized outcome regression model and then constructs a rate doubly-robust estimator based on the influence function of the QTE. However, as shown in Kallus et al. (2024) and for the sake of completeness in Section 2.2, for the LDML estimator to be n−1/2-consistent, it demands certain strong assumptions on the rate of convergence of the initial QTE estimator, hence also on the regularity of the propensity score; see Proposition 2. Ai et al. (2021) bypass the two-step procedure and estimate the QTE by using only the propensity score. But unlike the LDML estimator, the QTE estimator of Ai et al. (2021) fails to be rate double-robust. It is then natural to raise the following question: 

Can estimators of QTE or other more general implicitly-defined causal parameters be constructed that improve upon existing ones based on influence functions, in terms of convergence rates and the required assumptions on the initial estimators? 

We provide a positive answer to the above inquiry by going beyond the classical first-order semiparametric theory, in a sense formalized later in the paper. For implicitly defined parameters, however, the literature beyond first-order estimators is rather scarce, with the only exception being Section 6 of Robins et al. (2016), which touches on the issue without providing a treatment with further technical details. Also, the regression slope in a partially linear model was considered in 3Robins et al. (2016) as a leading example, which has an explicit form 2. Our paper partially fills this gap. To strike a balance between the generality and practical utility of our results, we opt for considering a broad class of parameters defined via generic moment equations with possibly non-smooth, non-separable generalized residual functions (see (1) ). This formulation accommodates discrete, continuous, and more complicated treatment types in a unified way, and it aligns with a growing econometrics literature that represents causal targets through generic moments. For example, Ao et al. (2021) use a generic moment function applied to counterfactual distributions under multivalued treatments. Within this unified formulation, we cover a wide range of parameters encountered in applied causal inference, including ATE, QTE, and more generally the General Treatment Models (GTMs) recently coined in the econometrics literature (Ai et al., 2021; Chen and Xie, 2025). Extending the HOIF framework to parameters of GTMs is thus instrumental towards building a broadly applicable point and interval estimation scheme for parameters encountered in econometrics. 

Summary of main results and contributions 

Compared to the HOIF theory for explicitly defined parameters, the following challenges remain to be addressed, which constitute the main contributions of our paper. (1) We derive HOIFs for causal parameters implicitly defined through GTMs, facilitating the construction of improved higher-order estimating equations, compared to state-of-the-art estimators based on the first-order theory, such as the LDML estimator. While we mainly analyze estimators solving second-order estimating equations, we refer to them as higher-order estimators to avoid introducing additional terminology and we also present the full m-th order construction in Supplementary Material Section S3. Among the parameters of GTMs, the QTE with binary treatment is one of the most commonly encountered examples in practice. We obtain a n−1/2-consistent estimator for QTE under the weakest Hölder smoothness assumptions on the nuisance parameters to date, almost matching the minimal smoothness assumptions for ATE to be n−1/2-estimable. (2) State-of-the-art first-order estimators require an initial estimator of the target parameter to estimate the nuisance parameters. This extra step incurs an additional rate-condition on the initial estimator and, consequently, on certain nuisance parameter(s). As we will see in Section 3.4, for the QTE with binary treatment, our proposed higher-order estimators can drastically relax this rate-condition, highlighting another advantage of higher-order estimators (see Table 1 in Section 3.4). (3) The original formulation of GTMs by Ai et al. (2021) assumes a parametric specification for the target parameters. When the treatment takes finitely many discrete values, finite-dimensional GTMs are effectively nonparametric. However, in many modern applications, treatment variables are often continuous. It may then be necessary to consider infinite-dimensional GTMs to avoid model misspecification bias; or otherwise, the finite-dimensional GTMs can at best be interpreted as a projection of the true causal or structural parameters onto a parametric                           

> 2Partial-linear semiparametric regression model reads as Y=βA +b(X) + ε, where εhas mean zero and ε⊥⊥A, X .Then the regression slope β≡E[cov (A, Y |X)] /E[var (A|X)] is simply the ratio between the expected conditional covariance of A, Y given Xand the expected conditional variance of Agiven X, two well-studied examples of parameters, often referred to as doubly-robust functionals (Rotnitzky et al., 2021).

4model. To our knowledge, Colangelo and Lee (2026) and Bonvini and Kennedy (2022) are among the first to consider estimating the average dose-response function (see Example 7 later) using higher-order estimators when the treatment is continuous. To incorporate such scenarios, we extend our higher-order estimators to nonparametric GTMs, thereby generalizing the results of Colangelo and Lee (2026) and Bonvini and Kennedy (2022) to include, for example, the quantile dose-response function (see Example 8 later). Before proceeding, we illustrate the value of incorporating HOIF theory for GTM parameters through a simulation study; full details are provided in Sections 3.1 and 5.1. For clarity of exposition, we present the discussion at a high level and defer some technical rigor to later sections. Figure 1 reports the simulation results. The parameter of interest β∗ is the QTE at the 25% quantile, with binary treatment and a one-dimensional covariate that suffices to control for confounding. The histograms display the simulated distribution of the centered estimator, ̂ β − β∗. In the left panel of Figure 1, we adopt the aforementioned state-of-the-art LDML estimator (Kallus et al., 2024), with nuisance parameters (see Example 2 later) estimated by neural networks (Chen et al., 2024a). We design the simulation such that the nuisance parameters have low smoothness in Hölder sense (with smoothness close to s = 0 .4). As a result, even LDML with neural networks fails to deliver an estimator that is close to being unbiased, which is not too surprising given the empirical evidence on the difficulty of fitting functions of low regularity by neural networks in practice (Xu et al., 2022). In contrast, the right panel of Figure 1 shows that, after centering by the true QTE, our HOIF-based estimator is well centered around zero, though with slightly higher variance than the LDML estimator. This empirical result demonstrates that the HOIF framework can be a useful tool even when modern neural networks are employed for nonparametric first-step estimation, complementing state-of-the-art methods based on classical semiparametric theory. 

Figure 1: Comparison between the first-order (LDML) estimator and the higher-order estimator (HOE) of QTE. The histograms over 1000 Monte Carlo simulations are displayed, and the red curves are the corresponding normal probability density function with the same mean and variance as the Monte Carlo mean and variance. 51.2 A survey of related works 

Our work is related to several strands of literature in econometrics and statistics. We now give a brief (and very likely incomplete) survey. (1) Two-step procedures. The problem studied in this paper is related to classical and modern semiparametric two-step estimation in econometrics, where some first-stage nuisance com-ponents may be indexed by the target parameter. Classical semiparametric theory typically imposes conditions under which the first-stage bias is asymptotically negligible, so that the limiting distribution of the target estimator is not affected; see, among others, Newey (1990); Newey and McFadden (1994); Chen et al. (2003) and the references therein. More recent work develops estimation and inference procedures that remain valid when first-stage bias is non-negligible. For example, Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) and Cattaneo et al. (2019) study two-step estimators where the first-stage bias can enter the asymptotic distribution of the target parameter, using kernel-based and linear-regression-based nuisance estimators, respectively. Specifically, Cattaneo et al. (2019) consider a setting with many covariates, where the first-stage nuisance estimator is obtained by linear regression. They show that a non-negligible leave-in bias arises when the same sample is used for both steps, and propose jackknife-type methods to consistently estimate and remove this bias, yielding a bias-corrected estimator. Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) develop a small-bandwidth asymptotic framework for kernel-based first-step estimators, where the smoothing bias is not assumed negligible. They show that the standard nonparametric bootstrap automatically corrects for this bias, so that percentile confidence intervals achieve correct coverage. When the asymptotic bias cannot be consistently estimated, Cavaliere et al. (2024) propose a distributional adjustment to restore bootstrap validity. Their method first obtains a bootstrap p-value, which is not asymptotically uniform due to the bias. They show that this p-value converges to a non -uniform limit that does not depend on the bias. By applying a probability integral transformation estimated via a second bootstrap, they obtain a corrected p-value that is asymptotically uniform, thus restoring valid inference without directly estimating the bias. Cattaneo et al. (2025) develop higher-order distributional approximations for kernel-based estimators of the density-weighted average derivative. By employing Edgeworth expansions, they refine the large-sample approximation of the estimator’s distribution, which leads to improved finite-sample inference, particularly enhancing the coverage accuracy of confidence intervals. Another broad class of approaches focuses on estimators with a small bias property (SBP) (Newey et al., 2004), meaning that the bias of the target estimator vanishes faster than the bias of the first-stage nuisance estimators. Newey et al. (2004) shows that a class of semiparametric estimators based on twicing kernels satisfy this property. Tackling a similar problem and motivated by the HOIF theory, Chen et al. (2024b) proposed moment-based √n-consistent estimators of a class of causal parameters even when the nuisance parameters, assumed to be generalized linear models, are not consistently estimable. (2) Influence functions and semiparametric inference. Our work builds on and extends semi-parametric and nonparametric inference based on influence functions, which play a central role in semiparametric efficiency theory. Influence-function-based estimation is a classical topic in semiparametric theory and has been studied for decades. For example, Schick (1986) develops a general method for constructing asymptotically efficient estimators in semipara-metric models. Newey (1990) provides an introduction to semiparametric efficiency bounds, 6discussing their nature, calculation, and application in constructing semiparametric estimators and deriving their limiting distributions. For a more detailed and comprehensive treatment of influence functions for general semiparametric models and statistical inference, see Bickel et al. (1998). As mentioned, our results can be viewed as a further development of the higher-order generalization of the classical influence-function-based approaches (Robins et al., 2008). For binary treatment, Robins et al. (1994) and Hahn (1998) derived the efficient influence functions for ATE and ATT (average treatment effect on the treated). Cattaneo (2010) considered a general treatment effect model with possibly over-identified and non-smooth moment conditions under multi-valued treatments, covering a broad class of treatment-effect parameters including average and quantile treatment effects, and established the corresponding efficiency bounds. Building on these efficiency results, the relevant literature focuses on developing practical estimators for treatment effects; see, for example, Hirano et al. (2003) and the references therein. Recent work also leverages influence functions to obtain valid inference with high-dimensional or machine-learned nuisance components (Chernozhukov et al., 2018). Colangelo and Lee (2026) further extend DML-type ideas to kernel-based inference on the average dose–response function under continuous treatments. Rotnitzky et al. (2021) and Chernozhukov et al. (2022) characterized a large class of explicitly-defined parameters, including ATE and ATT, for which DML-type estimators can be constructed based on their influence functions. Newey and Robins (2018) developed refined DML-type estimators that achieve faster convergence rates under smoothness classes with different nuisance parameters estimated from different independent subsamples. Papers mentioned above mainly concern explicitly-defined parameters, including ATE and ATT. In terms of implicitly-defined parameters, Firpo (2007) studies efficient influence functions for QTE with binary treatment. Ao et al. (2021) develop a unified moment-based framework for multi-valued treatments, covering means, quantiles, and distribution functions, and propose an efficient influence function based estimator for multivalued treatment effects for the treated. Their approach also enables decomposition analysis, separating wage structure effects from composition effects. Applying their method to evaluate the Workforce Investment Act (WIA) program, they find that heterogeneity in participation levels is an important dimension for evaluating social programs, demonstrating the practical relevance of their methods for policy evaluation. Complementarily, Ai et al. (2021) provide a unified framework for continuous treatments and derive efficient influence functions for general treatment models. As mentioned, more recently, Kallus et al. (2024) propose the LDML estimator for the QTE, along the line of DML estimators.

1.4 Organization 

The remainder of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 introduces the observation scheme and the general treatment model (GTM) and reviews the state-of-the-art estimators for GTM based on (first-order) influence functions from the standard semiparametric theory. Section 3 presents the main results: In Section 3.1, using the QTE as an illustration, we motivate the importance of introducing higher-order estimators to reduce bias and provide the intuition of why HOIFs can be used for bias correction; Section 3.2 introduces the HOIF-based estimator for finite-dimensional GTM parameters, and Section 3.3 establishes its statistical properties; Section 3.4 specializes the results obtained in Section 3.3 to QTE. Section 4 addresses the estimation of infinite-dimensional GTM parameters, allowing greater modeling flexibility. Section 5 presents empirical studies of the HOIF estimator. In particular, Section 5.1 investigates the finite-sample performance of the HOIF estimator in low-regularity settings using simulation experiments, followed by a real data analysis in Section 5.2. Section 6 concludes the paper with a discussion of caveats and future directions. Proofs and less essential technical results are collected in the Supplementary Material. 82 Problem Setup and Review of Existing Results 

2.1 Causal parameters defined via general treatment models 

Let T ∈ T be a general treatment variable that can be discrete, continuous, or a mix of both. Let 

Y (t) ∈ Y ⊂ R denote the real-valued potential outcome when T is intervened/set to t, and let Y

be the observed outcome. To control for confounding, we observe a vector of compactly supported baseline covariates X ∈ X ≡ [0 , 1] d ⊆ Rd, for some integer d ≥ 1. Let P denote the joint distribution of the observed data (X, T, Y ) and E the corresponding expectation. Let Γ : Y × T × B → Rp be a known generalized residual function, which can be nonsmooth and non-separable. We are interested in the parameter β∗ = ( β∗

(GTM), a unified moment-based framework for binary, multi-valued, and continuous treatments that covers means, quantiles, distribution functions, and various treatment effects. Similar frameworks are also studied in Cattaneo (2010); Ao et al. (2021); Ai et al. (2021). Since (1) involves the potential outcome Y (t), we need to impose the following identification assumptions to turn (1) into a functional of the observed data distribution. Let pT |X (t | x) denote the conditional density/mass function of T given X = x, referred to as the generalized propensity score .

Assumption 1. We assume the following: (i) (Consistency) Y ≡ Y (T ); (ii) (Positivity) The generalized propensity score satisfies 0 < c 1 ≤ pT |X (t|x) ≤ c2 < ∞ for all t ∈ T with ω(t) > 0, for constants c1, c 2; (iii) (Unconfoundedness) Y (t) ⊥⊥ T | X for all t ∈ T.Assumption 1 comprises the standard identification conditions for causal effects in observational studies. Notably, the stated positivity assumption allows practitioners to choose weight tailored for regions of T where positivity holds. Under Assumption 1, β∗, which is defined through the potential-outcome moment equations (1) , is identified as the unique solution to the following weighted moment equations based on the observed data distribution: 

E{ξ(X, T )Γ( Y, T, β∗)} = 0 , (2) where the function ξ(x, t ) := ω(t)/pT |X (t|x) is also called the stabilized weight in the epidemiology literature (Robins et al., 2000). In this paper, we focus on two types of ω: (1) ω(·) ≡ pT (·), the marginal distribution of the treatment T , which yields a finite-dimensional causal parameter (see Section 2.2 and Section 3); (2) ω(·) ≡ pT (·)δt(·), the point mass at a particular value t of T , which yields a nonparametric causal curve as a function of t (see Section 4). Our framework can be readily extended to the case where ω(·) is any known function of T or additionally depends on X (see Example 5), but we focus on these two choices to avoid unnecessary complications. 9We present several commonly encountered examples of treatment effect parameters defined via GTM (1) , demonstrating the importance of developing new and improved statistical procedures for this general class.

= inf {q : P(Y (1) ≤ q) ≥ τ }, which are the τ th 

quantiles of the potential outcomes. QTE has been studied in Chernozhukov and Hansen (2005); Firpo (2007); Kallus et al. (2024), among others. We will revisit QTE in Sections 3.2 and 3.4 later as the most important concrete example of GTM parameters. 

Example 3 (Distributional Treatment Effect (DTE)) . For any y ∈ R, let Γ( Y (t), t, β) = (( 1{Y (t) ≤

y} − β0)(1 − t), (1{Y (t) ≤ y} − β1)t)⊤, ω(t) = pT (t), and T = {0, 1}, where β = ( β0, β 1)⊤. The solution to (1) is β∗ 

> 0

= P(Y (0) ≤ y) and β∗ 

> 1

= P(Y (1) ≤ y). DTE has been studied in Chernozhukov et al. (2013) and Donald and Hsu (2014), among others. 

Example 4 (α-Expected Shortfall (ES)) . Fix α ∈ (0 , 1) , let T = {0, 1} and set ω(t) ≡ pT (t). Define 

Γ(Y (t), t, β) = t ·

is the associated expected shortfall. Expected shortfall and related tail-risk functionals are widely used in policy learning in econometrics and related areas; see, e.g., Patton et al. (2019); Fan et al. (2025). Up to this point, all the examples have set ω(·) ≡ pT (·). In the next example, ω(·) is taken as a known function of T .

Example 5 (Stochastic Intervention Effect) . The weighting function ω(t) can be further generalized to depend on the covariates, i.e., ω(t, x ). When ω(t, x ) is a known, user-specified density function of 

T , β∗ corresponds to the stochastic (dynamical) intervention effect (Kennedy, 2019). 

Example 6 (Marginal Structural Models) . In its most common formulation, marginal structural models (MSMs) (Robins et al., 2000) postulate a parametric model for the marginal mean of potential outcomes: 

EY (t) = g(t; β∗). (3) Model (3) can be rewritten as Model (1) by letting Γ( Y (t), t, β∗) = Y (t) − g(t; β∗). The weight-ing function ω needs to be arbitrary if (3) is taken as part of the modeling assumptions. See Supplementary Material Section S1 for further discussions. 10 If we set the weighting function ω(·) in (1) as the product of the marginal density function of 

T and the Dirac delta function pT (·)δt(·) for each possible treatment value t ∈ T, then the target parameter β∗ ≡ β∗(·) ∈ R∞ becomes a function of t, that is, an infinite-dimensional ( p = ∞)parameter. In Section 4, we further develop our approach for such infinite-dimensional GTM parameters. Notably, this framework encompasses two important infinite-dimensional parameters: the average and quantile dose–response functions (ADRF and QDRF). 

Example 7. [Average Dose-Response Function (ADRF)] When Γ( Y (t), t, β ) = Y (t) − β and ω(·) = 

pT (·)δt(·), the solution to (1) is β∗(t) = E[Y (t)] . The ADRF has been widely studied in the literature; see Kennedy et al. (2017), Su et al. (2019) and Colangelo and Lee (2026), among others. 

Example 8. [Quantile Dose-Response Function (QDRF)] When Γ( Y (t), t, β ) = τ − 1{Y (t) ≤ β} and 

ω(·) = pT (·)δt(·), the solution to (1) is β∗(t) = inf {q : P(Y (t) ≤ q) ≥ τ }, the τ th quantile of the potential outcome Y (t). The QDRF has been studied by Galvao and Wang (2015) and Su et al. (2019). 

Remark 1. Finally, though not our main focus, we note that other unknown weighting functions 

ω can also be considered. For example, when T ∈ T = R is a continuous treatment variable with probability density function pT (t), let Γ( Y (t), t, β 0) = Y (t) + β0t and ω(t) = ∂tpT (t), the derivative of pT (t). Under Assumption 1 and using integration by parts, the solution to (1) becomes

(2025) under a randomized experiment, i.e., with a known propensity score function pT |X ; see Remark 6 for further discussion. Extending the analysis to observational studies is a topic worth pursuing in future work. ■

2.2 Review of existing results based on influence functions 

As mentioned, we take ω(·) ≡ pT (·) here and in Section 3, so that ξ(x, t ) = pT (t)/pT |X (t|x). Before presenting our new results, we first review state-of-the-art estimators of β∗ based on influence functions from the (first-order) semiparametric efficiency theory. For convenience, we define 

bβ(x, t ) := E{Γ( Y, T, β)|X = x, T = t}. Since Γ( Y, T, β) essentially plays the same role as the outcome Y in ATE, we refer to bβ(·, ·) as the generalized outcome regression model . We denote by 

θβ := ( ξ, b β) the collection of nuisance parameters for a fixed β. It is worth noting that β may be non-separable from the nuisance function bβ and can enter in a nonlinear manner. From (2) and by the law of iterated expectations, β∗ solves the equation 

ψ(β) ≡ ψ(θβ) := E{ξ(X, T )Γ( Y, T, β)} = E{ξ(X, T )bβ(X, T )} = 0 . (4) Ai et al. (2021) developed the semiparametric efficiency theory for β∗. Since the rest of this paper relies on this preliminary result, we reproduce it below in Proposition 1:

Assumption 2. ξ(·, ·) and bβ(·, ·) are assumed to be Hölder smooth functions with smoothness indices 

s1 and s2 respectively, for every β ∈ B. We let s := ( s1 + s2)/2 denote the average smoothness of the two nuisance parameters. The nuisance estimators ̂ ξ and ̂ bβ converge to ξ and bβ respectively at the minimax optimal rates ( n− s1 

> d+2 s1

and n− s2 

> d+2 s2

) in L2(P)-distance. The Hölder smoothness conditions in Assumption 2 are standard for quantifying the complexity of nuisance functions in nonparametric and semiparametric theory. The stated minimax rates are attainable by classical series, sieve, and kernel estimators under appropriate choice of tuning parameters such as the bandwidth or the number of basis functions; see, e.g., Stone (1982). Assumption 2 is used to translate the high-level rate conditions on the nuisance estimators into primitive smoothness requirements; see the discussion following Proposition 2, Remark 4, and the discussion below Assumption 9. In the binary QTE example in Section 3.4, this condition is specialized as Assumption 12 and is used in the proof of Theorem 5. As noted in the Introduction, a major statistical challenge in applying IF (1)  

> ψ

to construct a first-order estimator of β is that the nuisance parameter bβ itself depends on the unknown parameter β.Directly applying Proposition 1 therefore requires estimating the entire nuisance function, meaning that bβ needs to be estimated for every candidate β. One simple way to bypass this difficulty is to first obtain an initial estimator βinit close to β∗, for example, by solving the empirical version of (4) and then substitute bβinit into the influence function IF (1)  

> ψ

. This approach is referred to as localized debiased machine learning (LDML), recently developed in Kallus et al. (2024). To formally construct this first-order estimator ̂ β(1) , we define ̂

where O′ = ( Y ′, T ′, X ′) is an independent copy of O = ( Y, T, X ).We construct the first-order estimator ̂ ψ(1) (β) of ψ(β) by the following second-order U -statistic (similar construction also appeared in Bonvini et al. (2022)): ̂

For convenience, we assume that the nuisance estimators ̂ ξ,̂ bβinit and the initial estimator βinit of β∗ are all computed using an independent separate sample of size n, referred to as the nuisance sample . The U -statistic operator Un,m , for any 

m ≥ 2, is taken only over the main sample throughout the paper. All results are stated conditional on the nuisance sample. For brevity, we often suppress this dependence in our notation, particularly the dependence on βinit . To improve efficiency, one can follow the standard cross-fitting procedure of Chernozhukov et al. (2018) by flipping the roles of the main and nuisance samples to construct a cross-fit version of the estimator ̂ ψ(1) (β). However, to avoid notation clutter, we will stick to the estimator without cross-fitting.

The statistical properties of ̂ β(1) are formally stated in Proposition 2 below. This result is analogous to Theorem 1 of Kallus et al. (2024). A proof sketch is provided in Section 3.1, which also serves to motivate the introduction of our higher-order estimators.

Proposition 2 shows that, for ̂ β(1) to be n−1/2-consistent and asymptotically normal, the initial estimator βinit must converge to β∗ fast enough to accurately estimate the nuisance function bβ. If 

βinit is obtained by solving the empirical version of (4) using estimated stabilized weights ̂ ξ that converge to the true ξ at rate rn,ξ , then rn, βinit = O(rn,ξ ). Thus, unless rn, βinit converges to zero at an even faster rate than rn,ξ (a highly unlikely scenario), we require both rn,ξ and rn,b to be oP(n−1/4).Under Assumption 2, these rate conditions imply that 2s1/d  

> 1+2 s1/d

> 0.5 and s1/d

> 1+2 s2/d

> 0.5.In the special case s1 = s2 = s, this further reduces to s/d > 0.5, under which both nuisance parameters even satisfy the Donsker condition. However, inspired by the known optimal rate for ATE estimation under Hölder -type nuisance assumptions (Robins et al., 2023), it is reasonable to conjecture that s/d ≥ 0.25 suffices to guarantee a n−1/2-consistent estimator for finite-dimensional parameters of GTM under reasonable regularity conditions on the generalized residual function Γ.The remainder of this section is devoted to constructing a higher-order estimator of β∗ requiring much relaxed Hölder -type smoothness assumptions on the nuisance parameters and the initial βinit ,by leveraging the HOIF framework. 

# 3 Higher-Order Estimators: The Finite-Dimensional Case 

In this section, we present our main methodological and theoretical results on new higher-order estimators for finite-dimensional GTM parameters. We first motivate the need for higher-order estimators beyond the first-order estimator ̂ β(1) through a simple numerical experiment in Section 3.1, accompanied by a brief and intuitive introduction to HOIF theory. Section 3.2 then discusses how to construct higher-order estimators for GTM parameters, and Section 3.3 studies their statistical properties. Finally, Section 3.4 specializes these general results to the QTE. 13 3.1 Motivation for higher-order estimators: The QTE example 

Before proceeding, we provide a more technically rigorous motivation for constructing higher-order estimators for GTMs, complementing the empirical motivation based on the simulation results in Figure 1 in the Introduction. We focus on β∗ 

> 1

, the τ -quantile of the potential outcome Y (1) , which was shown to be a GTM parameter in Example 2. As discussed in Remark 2 and Proposition 2 (following Kallus et al. (2024)), we can analyze the bias of the LDML estimator ̂ β(1) by examining the bias of the first-order moment equation ̂ ψ(1) (β1) for ψ(β1) at a given β1:

E{̂ψ(1) (β1) − ψ(β1)} = E

[{ P(T = 1 | X)̂

Recall that ̂ ξ and ̂ bβ1,init are constructed on the nuisance sample, whereas the expectation is taken with respect to the main sample. Hence, conditional on the nuisance sample, ̂ ξ and ̂ bβ1,init can be treated as fixed functions, and the bias 

E[{̂ξ(X, T ) − ξ(X, T )}{ bβ1 (X, T ) −̂ bβ1,init (X, T )}] captures only the approximation bias of these fixed estimators, not the sampling variability in the nuisance estimates. Decomposing bβ1 −̂ bβ1,init as (bβ1 − bβ1,init ) + ( bβ1,init −̂ bβ1,init ) and applying the triangle inequality, we can bound the bias of ̂ ψ(1) (β1) under suitable regularity conditions on β1 7 → bβ1 as follows: 

∥̂ξ − ξ∥P,2(∥̂bβ1,init − bβ1,init ∥P,2 + ∥β1,init − β1∥). (7) Since β1,init depends on ̂ ξ, the bias of ̂ ψ(1) (β1) is consequently bounded by

Furthermore, the bias of ̂ ψ(1) (β1) can be decomposed as 

E{̂ ψ(1) (β1) − ψ(β1)} = B ψ,k (β1) + E{Π⊥(̂ ξ − ξ | ¯ϕk)( X, T ) · Π⊥(bβ1 −̂ bβ1,init | ¯ϕk)( X, T )},

where Π⊥ denotes the orthocomplement of the projection and following Robins et al. (2008), we denote the second term in the decomposition as TB ψ,k (β1). TB ψ,k (β1) is referred to as the 

truncation bias in Robins et al. (2008), because it represents the remaining bias, after “truncating” the potentially infinite-dimensional residual functions ̂ ξ − ξ and bβ1 −̂ bβ1,init up to a finite dimension 

k by the basis ¯ϕk. Under appropriate assumptions on ̂ ξ − ξ and bβ1 −̂ bβ1,init and a suitable choice of 

¯ϕk (to be clarified later), TB ψ,k (β1) can be made negligible compared to sampling variability. The key idea of our higher-order estimation approach is to decompose the bias of the first-order estimator ̂ ψ(1) (β1) of ψ(β1) into two parts Bψ,k (β1)+ TB ψ,k (β1), so that Bψ,k (β1) can be estimated at a sufficiently fast rate by an estimator ̂ Bψ,k and TB ψ,k (β1) is negligible. We then solve a bias-reduced estimating equation ̂ ψ(1) −̂ Bψ,k (typically a U -statistic) to construct the higher-order estimator ̂

β(2) 1 . We summarize the construction of ̂ β(2) 1 in Figure 2. As mentioned in the Introduction, Figure 1 displays the histograms of different QTE estimators after centered on the true QTE value (i.e., the estimate minus the truth) in a simulation setting where nuisance estimation errors ∥̂ ξ − ξ∥P,2 and ∥̂bβ1,init − bβ1,init ∥P,2 are designed to be large. In the left panel, the LDML estimator exhibits a noticeable bias relative to its sampling variability. In contrast, the right panel shows that the histogram of our higher-order estimators is much more centered around zero, indicating a negligible bias relative to its sampling variability. The variance of the higher-order estimator is only slightly larger than that of the LDML estimator. These results demonstrate that the proposed higher-order estimator can be useful not only in theory but also in practice. 15 3.2 Higher-order estimators: The general case 

In the general case, we begin by noting that the bias of the first-order estimator ̂ ψ(1) (β) for the moment equation ψ(β) can also be written as the mean of a product of two nuisance estimation residuals: 

E{̂ ψ(1) (β) − ψ(β)} = E[{̂ξ(X, T ) − ξ(X, T )}{ bβ(X, T ) −̂ bβinit (X, T )}]. (9) To reduce this bias, we project each residual onto a size-k dictionary ¯ϕk := {ϕ1, · · · , ϕ k} functions. Specifically, we select ¯ϕk ≡ ¯zkx ⊗ ¯wkt as the tensor product of a size-kx dictionary ¯zkx := {z1, · · · , zkx }

over X and a size-kt dictionary ¯wkt := {w1, · · · , wkt } over T, as in general T is no longer binary. Using the same projection arguments as in Section 3.1, the bias decomposes as 

E{̂ ψ(1) (β) − ψ(β)} = E{Π( ̂ξ − ξ | ¯ϕk)( X, T ) · Π( bβ −̂ bβinit | ¯ϕk)( X, T )}

+ E{Π⊥(̂ ξ − ξ | ¯ϕk)( X, T ) · Π⊥(bβ −̂ bβinit | ¯ϕk)( X, T )}

=: B ψ,k (β) + TB ψ,k (β),

where Π denotes projection onto the span of ¯ϕk and Π⊥ is its orthocomplement. Moreover, 

Bψ,k (β) = E[{̂ξ(X, T ) − ξ(X, T )} ¯ϕk(X, T )⊤]Σ −1 

> k

E[ ¯ϕk(X, T ){bβ(X, T ) −̂ bβinit (X, T )}]. (10) A key insight underlying the improved second-order estimator is that the bias term Bψ,k (β) admits an unbiased sample analogue, if Σk is known. First, by the law of iterated expectations, 

E[ ¯ϕk(X, T ){bβ(X, T ) −̂ bβinit (X, T )}] = E[ ¯ϕk(X, T ){Γ( Y, T, β) −̂ bβinit (X, T )}].

Next, recall that ξ(X, T ) = pT (T )/pT |X (T | X). Weighting by ξ transforms the joint distribution of 

(X, T ) into the product of their marginal distributions. Indeed, 

E[ξ(X, T ) ¯ϕk(X, T )] = 

∫∫ 

¯ϕk(x, t ) pT (t) pX (x)d tdx. 

This identity is crucial: it shows that the expectation of ξ(X, T ) ¯ϕk(X, T ) can be estimated by averaging ¯ϕk(Xi, T j ) over independent pairs (Xi, T j ) from the main sample. Combining these two observations, we obtain an unbiased estimator of Bψ,k (β) as the following third-order U -statistic:

.

Formally analyzing the statistical properties of ˜β(2) and ̂ β(2) requires a detailed examination of the impact of the initial estimator βinit and heavy use of U -processes theory, since they are third-order Z-estimators. We will make these intuitive arguments precise in Section 3.3. 

Remark 3. The initial nuisance estimators ̂ ξ and ̂ bβinit can be obtained by any flexible method (e.g., kernel smoothing, series estimation, or machine learning) and are treated as fixed functions conditional on the nuisance sample. The dictionary ¯ϕk is introduced solely for the purpose of bias correction: we project the residual functions ̂ ξ − ξ and bβ −̂ bβinit onto the linear span of ¯ϕk, and the bias correction term ˜Bψ,k (β) estimates the inner product of these projections. The improvement comes from choosing ¯ϕk with sufficient approximation power for these residuals; in particular, the dictionary used for bias correction should be richer than or different from any dictionary used in the initial nuisance estimation. ■

17 3.3 Asymptotic properties of higher-order estimators: The general case 

En route to deriving statistical properties of our proposed second-order estimators, we further require the following regularity conditions. When stating these conditions, we introduce positive and finite constants c3, . . . , c 8 that do not vary with n.

Assumption 3. The support T of the treatment variable T is a compact subset of R.

Assumption 4. (i) The parameter space B is a compact subset of Rp and the true parameter β∗ is in the interior of B. (ii) β∗ is the unique solution to the population first-order estimating equation 

ψ(β) = 0 and for every ϵ > 0, inf ∥β−β∗∥≥ ϵ ∥ψ(β)∥ > 0.

Assumption 5. (i) ψ(β) is differentiable with respect to β at any β ∈ B; denote its derivative with respect to β as ∇βψ(β) ∈ Rp×p. (ii) Each component of ∇βψ(β) is continuous at β∗. (iii) The eigenvalues of ∇βψ(β∗) are bounded between constants c3 and c4.

Assumption 6. (i) sup β∈B ∥bβ(·, ·)∥∞ ≲ 1, sup β∈B ∥̂ bβ(·, ·)∥∞ ≲ 1 and ∥̂ ξ(·, ·)∥∞ ≲ 1. (ii) For any 

β1, β2 ∈ B, we have ∥bβ1 (X, T ) − bβ2 (X, T )∥P,2 ≲ ∥β1 − β2∥. (iii) For any (x, t ) ∈ X × T, we have 

c5 ≤ pX (x) ≤ c6 and c5 ≤ pT (t) ≤ c6.

Assumption 7. (i) The function class {Γ( ·, ·, β) : β ∈ B} is of VC type with fixed characteristic 

(A, v ). (ii) sup β∈B ∥Γ( ·, ·, β)∥∞ ≲ 1. (iii) There exists some constant α0 ∈ (0 , 1] such that, for any 

β1, β2 ∈ B, any small δ > 0, E1/2[sup || β1−β2|| ≲δ ∥Γ( Y, T, β1) − Γ( Y, T, β2)∥2] ≲ δα0 .

Assumption 8. (i) For each j, the jth basis functions zj (·) and wj (·) are supported on sets of probability at most c7/k x and c7/k t, respectively. Let SX,j and ST,j denote the supports of 

zj (·) and wj (·), respectively. (ii) At any point (x, t ) ∈ X × T, at most c8 elements of ¯zkx (x)

and ¯wkt (t) are simultaneously non-zero. (iii) For every kx and kt, and for any unit vectors 

a = ( a1, . . . , a kx ) ∈ Rkx and b = ( b1, . . . , b kt ) ∈ Rkt , we have a⊤ ∫ 

> SX,j

¯zkx (x)¯zkx (x)⊤dxa ≳ a2 

> j

and 

b⊤ ∫ 

> ST,j

¯wkt (t) ¯wkt (t)⊤dtb ≳ b2 

> j

. (iv) There exist two sequences of constants ζx(kx) and ζt(kt) such that ∥¯wkt (·)∥∞ ≤ ζt(kt) ≲ √kt, ∥¯zkx (·)∥∞ ≤ ζx(kx) ≲ √kx, and ζ(k) := ζx(kx)ζt(kt). (v) The dimension k = kxkt satisfies k → ∞ and k = o(n2) as n → ∞ .Assumption 3 requires that the treatment T be bounded. This condition is imposed to simplify the analysis, as most existing technical tools for U -statistics and U -processes require the U -statistic kernel to be bounded. We expect that it could be weakened to light-tailed conditions (Chakrabortty and Kuchibhotla, 2025), but this is not the main focus of our paper. Assumptions 4 and 5 are standard identification and regularity conditions for Z-estimators. Assumption 6 imposes sufficient regularity conditions on the nuisance functions and their estimators. In particular, Assumption 6(iii), together with Assumption 1(ii), implies that ξ(·, ·) is bounded away from zero and infinity, which is commonly required in the existing literature; see Ai et al. (2021) and Kennedy et al. (2017), among others. Assumption 7(i) is a high-level complexity condition on the generalized residual function that defines GTM treatment effect parameters in (1) . In applications, this assumption shall be verified case-by-case. Assumptions 7(ii)-(iii) control the envelope functions, a standard requirement in 

M/Z -estimation when the criterion is non-smooth (see, e.g., Chen et al. 2003; Ai et al. 2021). Notably, Assumption 7(iii) is weaker than the Lipschitz continuity condition and accommodates various continuous and discontinuous functions. Fortunately, in various examples, these assumptions 18 are straightforward to verify. In Supplementary Material Section S2, we show that they hold for both ATE and QTE from Examples 1 and 2. Assumptions 8(i)–(ii) ensure control over the sup norm of series projection estimators, which are essential for analyzing the U -process and establishing variance bounds. These assumptions are satisfied by many commonly used sieve bases, including Cohen–Daubechies–Vial wavelet series, B-splines and local polynomial partition series; see Belloni et al., 2015; Liu et al., 2017; Cattaneo et al., 2020 for detailed discussions. Assumption 8(iii) imposes a mild local non-collinearity requirement on the basis functions and is verified by the basis functions mentioned above. This condition is also adopted in Cattaneo et al. (2020). Assumption 8(iv) is standard in the nonparametric regression literature and is verified for the basis functions mentioned above. Assumption 8(v) restricts the basis dimension k from growing too fast (slower than n2), a necessary condition to achieve asymptotic normality of the estimators. We now establish the consistency and convergence rate of the oracle second-order estimator ˜β(2) .

The complete proof of Theorem 2 is presented in Supplementary Material Section S7.3. We provide a proof sketch as follows. We begin by expanding ψ(β∗) around ˜β(2) :

0 = ψ(β∗) = ψ( ˜β(2) ) + ∇βψ(β†) · (β∗ − ˜β(2) ),

Term An is bias-related and is oP(1) under Condition (11) . Term Bn is a centered U -statistic, determining the asymptotic distribution of ˜β(2) . Its asymptotic normality follows from the classical martingale CLT (Bhattacharya and Ghosh, 1992). Since the kernel of this U -statistic depends on 

k, the linear component in the Hoeffding decomposition does not always dominate and thus the order of its asymptotic variance depends on the scaling between n and k, split into three different regimes as indicated in Theorem 2. The main technical challenge lies in analyzing the U -process term Cn, indexed by ˜β(2) and defined over a function class changing with the sample size n. Existing 

U -process maximal inequalities, such as those of Chen and Kato (2020), are conservative for our setting. To address this, we establish a refined local maximal inequality in Supplementary Material Section S6.2. 

Remark 6. Edgeworth expansions offer a different notion of optimality when non-asymptotic or finite sample performance is of concern. Cattaneo et al. (2025) study inference for the density-weighted average derivative effect (DWADE) parameter β∗ 

> 0

(see Remark 1) under a randomized experiment, i.e., the propensity score pT |X is known, using a kernel-based estimator ̂ β0. Their estimator is a second-order U -statistic with Hoeffding decomposition ̂ β0 − β∗ 

> 0

= ¯L + ¯Q, where ¯L and ¯Q are the linear and quadratic terms, respectively. Classical asymptotics impose bandwidth conditions that make ¯Q negligible, yielding an asymptotic linear representation. With smaller bandwidths, 

¯Q becomes non-negligible, leading to a quadratic approximation where V[̂ β0] = V[ ¯L] + V[ ¯Q] and 

V[̂ β0]−1/2(̂β0 − β∗ 

> 0

) d

→ N (0 , 1) . Using Edgeworth expansions, they derive refined distributional approximations that improve finite-sample confidence interval coverage. Our estimator ̂ ψ(2)  

> k

of the estimating equation is also a U -statistic whose asymptotic distribution depends on quadratic terms when k ≳ n. However, the goal of Cattaneo et al. (2025) is inference refinement (improving finite-sample coverage), while ours is bias correction to achieve rate-optimal estimation under low smoothness conditions. In future work, we can also consider such more refined higher-order distributional approximation if concerned about the finite-sample performance beyond the asymptotic distribution results in Theorem 2. ■

Remark 8. As suggested by a referee, the econometrics literature contains several related proposals for bias correction in two-step semiparametric settings, including jackknife- and bootstrap-based methods (Cattaneo et al., 2019; Cattaneo and Jansson, 2018). In Supplementary Material Section S5, we use the average treatment effect (ATE) as a concrete example to compare our approach with these alternatives. All three methods address the problem that first-stage nuisance estimation can bias the second-stage estimator. However, the sources of bias and the correction mechanisms differ. Cattaneo et al. (2019) and Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) aim to estimate the estimating equation ψ(β) itself, with nuisance functions estimated by linear regression and kernel methods, respectively. The resulting estimator is affected by leave-in bias due to the reuse of the same sample in both steps, particularly when the covariate dimension is large or the bandwidth is small. By contrast, we use sample splitting and focus on estimating the approximation bias defined in (9) . Our construction accommodates generic nuisance learners, such as logistic regression, random forests, or neural networks, because we use linear approximation only for the nuisance estimation residuals ̂ ξ − ξ

and ̂ bβinit − bβ, although to obtain sharp theoretical results we need to impose certain smoothness assumptions on ̂ ξ − ξ and ̂ bβinit − bβ (see Assumption 2). Despite these differences, there are important connections. Specifically, we show that, as Cattaneo et al. (2019) impose (approximately) linear assumptions on the nuisance functions whereas we do so only for the residuals of the nuisance estimates, both our target parameter and that of Cattaneo et al. (2019) reduce to a bilinear functional (Robins et al., 2007; Bruns-Smith et al., 2026) of the form 

Bψ,k (β) defined in (10) . We show that the jackknife- and bootstrap-based (under some assumption on the scaling between k and n) bias-corrected estimators are, respectively, exactly equivalent and asymptotically equivalent to our second-order U -statistic estimator ˜Bψ,k (β) of Bψ,k (β). Moreover, the HOIF framework provides a systematic way to construct higher-order U -statistic estimators even when Σk is unknown (see Supplementary Material Section S3), which we believe can also motivate similar higher-order jackknife- or bootstrap-based bias reduction methods (Koltchinskii, 2022). ■

Remark 9. As also noted by one referee, both Cavaliere et al. (2024) and our paper study settings where a non-negligible asymptotic bias affects the limiting distribution. However, the way how bias is handled differs. Cavaliere et al. (2024) consider statistics whose limiting distribution takes the form 

Tn = √n(̂θn − θ) d

→ Bn + Z1, where Z1 is centered normal and Bn is a non-vanishing asymptotic bias. They construct a bootstrap analogue T ∗ 

> n

:= √n(̂ θ∗ 

> n

−̂ θn), where ̂ θ∗ 

> n

is a bootstrap version of ̂

θn, such that T ∗ 

> n

−̂ Bn d∗

→ Z1, with ̂ Bn a bootstrap-based estimator of Bn. When the bootstrap fails to replicate the bias, i.e., ̂ Bn − Bn does not converge to zero but converges in distribution to a zero-mean random variable, the standard bootstrap p-value ̂

pn := P∗(T ∗ 

> n

≤ Tn) = P∗(T ∗ 

> n

−̂ Bn ≤ Tn −̂ Bn 

> )

= P∗(T ∗ 

> n

−̂ Bn ≤ (Tn − Bn) − (̂ Bn − Bn))

is no longer asymptotically uniform. Instead, its limiting distribution converges to a distribution H

that depends on the joint limiting distribution of (Tn −̂ Bn, T ∗ 

> n

−̂ Bn) but not on the unknown bias 

Bn itself. The key insight of Cavaliere et al. (2024) is to apply a prepivoting transformation: H(̂pn)

22 is asymptotically uniform. Since H is unknown, they estimate it via a double bootstrap to obtain ̂

Hn, and output the corrected p-value ˜pn := ̂ Hn(̂ pn). Therefore, their procedure does not directly estimate or remove the bias Bn; instead, it bypasses the bias by transforming the bootstrap p-value and estimates the distribution function H.In contrast, our approach directly estimates the bias itself. As shown in Section 3.2, we further decompose the first-order bias E[{̂ ξ(X, T ) − ξ(X, T )}{ bβ(X, T ) −̂ bβinit (X, T )}] into a leading bias component Bψ,k (β) and a truncation bias TB ψ,k (β) by projecting the residuals of the estimated nuisance functions onto a sieve basis ¯ϕk. The leading bias Bψ,k (β) is then unbiasedly estimated by a U -statistic-based correction term ˜Bψ,k (β) when Σk is known (see Section 3.2). Subtracting this correction term from the first-order estimating equation yields a bias-corrected estimator ̂ β(2) 

that achieves √n-consistency under weaker smoothness conditions than first-order methods (see Theorem 5 and the discussion in Section 3.4). ■

3.4 Application: QTE with a binary treatment over Hölder smoothness classes

The convergence rates for QTE in Theorem 5 match those for ATE established in Robins et al. (2016), which shows that these rates are minimax optimal for ATE. The proof of Theorem 5 is straightforward and proceeds as follows. Under the assumptions of Theorem 5 with an appropriate choice of basis ¯ϕk, we have 

∥Π⊥(̂ ξ − ξ | ¯ϕk)∥P,2 = O(k−s1/d ), ∥Π⊥(bβ1,init −̂ bβ1,init | ¯ϕk)∥P,2 = O(k−s2/d ),

because the Hölder -s2 smoothness of pY |X,T (β1 | · , T = 1) implies that bβ1 (·, 1) is also Hölder -s2

smooth for any β1 ∈ B. Furthermore, a direct calculation shows that ∇β1 bβ1 (·, 1) = −pY |X,T (β1|· , T =1) , which is also Hölder -s2 smooth for any β1 ∈ B. Combining these results with (13) , the bias of ̂

Remark 10. For ATE in Example 1, a direct calculation yields bβ(x, t ) = (( E(Y |X = x, T =0) − β0) · (1 − t), (E(Y |X = x, T = 1) − β1) · t)⊤ and ∇βbβ(x, t ) = diag (1 − t, t ). Each component of ∇βbβ is infinitely smooth. As a result, condition (11) imposes no further convergence rate requirements on the initial estimator βinit of β∗. ■

24 4 Higher-Order Estimators: Toward Infinite-Dimensional Parameters 

For continuous or high-dimensional treatments (Su et al., 2019), fixed-dimensional parameters in (1) are often inadequate. Motivated by Bonvini and Kennedy (2022) and Colangelo and Lee (2026), we extend our framework to infinite-dimensional GTMs by setting ω(·) ≡ pT (·)δt(·) and letting p → ∞ in (1) . One purpose of this section is to further advocate the fruitful philosophy of turning an infinite-dimensional problem into a finite but diverging-dimensional functional estimation problem (Kennedy et al., 2024). For notational convenience, we denote the infinite-dimensional GTM parameter as β∗ 

> t

≡ β∗(t) : T → R, a function of the treatment value T = t. Then β∗ 

> t

solves 

∫

> u∈T

E{Γ( Y (u), u, β ∗ 

> t

)}pT (u)δt(u)d u ≡ 0, ∀ t ∈ T.

Under Assumption 1, β∗ 

> t

is identified as the solution to: 

ψt(β) ≡ ψt(θβ ) := E {ξ(X, T )Γ( Y, T, β )δt(T )} ≡ 0, (14) where ξ(x, t ) = pT (t)/pT |X (t | x). Here Γ : Y × T × B → R is a known one-dimensional generalized residual function. To simplify notation, we maintain the conventions of Section 2: for any β ∈ B,we define bβ (x, t ) = E{Γ( Y, T, β ) | X = x, T = t}. It is worth noting that in this section we are interested in estimating the GTM parameter at some fixed treatment value T = t.

4.1 Higher-order estimators for infinite-dimensional parameters

# 5 Numerical Experiments 

5.1 Simulation studies 

In this section, we conduct a proof-of-concept simulation study comparing the performance of our proposed higher-order estimator with two competing methods that do not utilize the HOIF framework. We focus on estimating the QTE parameter with binary treatment, β∗ = ( β∗ 

> 0

, β ∗ 

> 1

)⊤, as described in Example 2. Recall that β∗ 

> 1

(resp. β∗ 

> 0

) denotes the τ -quantile of the treatment (resp. control) group. We choose τ = 25% in this simulation. Following Xu et al. (2022) and Liu et al. (2017), we simulate data from the following two data-generating mechanisms with different numbers of baseline covariates ( d = 1 for Case 1 and d = 4 for Case 2): 

• Case 1: 



X ∼ Uniform([ −1, 1]) ,T | X ∼ Bernoulli(expit( η(0 .5 · X; s))) ,Y (t) | X = (1 + 0 .3 · η(0 .5 · X; s)) · t + 0 .2 · ε, 

• Case 2: 



X = ( X1, . . . , X 4)⊤ ∼ Uniform([ −1, 1] 4),T | X ∼ Bernoulli(expit( η(0 .5 · X1; s))) ,Y (t) | X = (1 + 0 .3 · η(0 .5 · X1; s)) · t + 0 .2 · ε, 

where ε ∼ N (0 , 1) , expit (x) = 1 /(1 + exp (−x)) , η(x; s) = ∑ 

> j∈J,l ∈Z

2−j(s+0 .25) wj,l (x) with J =

{0, 3, 6, 9, 10 , 16 } and wj,l (·) is the D6 father wavelet functions. By construction, η(·; s) lies close to 28 the boundary of Hölder -smooth functions with smoothness index s. We note that this is only an approximation, as exact infinite basis expansions are infeasible in simulations. Cases 1 and 2 share the same true outcome regression and propensity score. The only difference is that Case 2 includes three extra non-contributing covariates. In the analysis, we do not assume such knowledge so the basis ¯zk depends on all four covariates by stacking the univariate spline basis blocks together with an intercept term ¯zk(x) = (1 , ¯zk1 (x1)⊤, ¯zk2 (x2)⊤, ¯zk3 (x3)⊤, ¯zk4 (x4)⊤)⊤, which yields a basis vector of dimension k = ∑4 

> j=1

kj + 1 .We vary s ∈ { 0.25 , 0.4, 0.6} to examine its impact on estimator performance and to validate the theoretical results derived earlier. For each combination of sample size n and smoothness level s,we run 1000 replications. In each replication, 2n observations are drawn and split equally into a nuisance sample and an estimation sample. We estimate β∗ using three methods: 

HOE: Our higher-order estimator is defined in Section 3.2. For each continuous covariate, we use a degree-1 B-spline expansion with the number of interior knots set to ⌈n/ 100 ⌉. As mentioned, we construct the basis ¯zk(x) by stacking the univariate spline basis blocks for all continuous covariates, together with an intercept term. Specifically, in Case 2, ¯zk(x) = (1 , ¯zk1 (x1)⊤, ¯zk2 (x2)⊤, ¯zk3 (x3)⊤, ¯zk4 (x4)⊤)⊤, with kj = ⌈n/ 100 ⌉ + 1 and total dimension k = 

> ∑4
> j=1

kj + 1 . On the nuisance sample, we estimate the stabilized weight function ̂ ξ via logistic regression, obtain the initial estimator βinit by stabilized weighting using ̂ ξ, and estimate ̂ bβinit 

also via logistic regression. 

GOE: The generalized optimization estimator of Ai et al. (2021), applied to both the nuisance and estimation samples. See Supplementary Material Section S9.1 for its explicit form. 

LDML: The localized debiased machine learning estimator of Kallus et al. (2024), implemented with the same settings as in Section 6.1 of that paper, where all nuisance functions are estimated via logistic regression. Both Figure 3 (for Case 1) and Figure 4 (for Case 2), displaying simulation results for the QTE β∗ 

> 1

− β∗ 

> 0

, show that when the smoothness parameter s is low (e.g., s = 0 .25 or more precisely 

s is close to 0.25 ), our higher-order estimator (HOE) substantially outperforms both GOE and LDML, achieving lower MSE and absolute bias, especially when the sample size n is relatively large. In particular, the higher-order estimator is empirically √n-consistent even when s is near 0.25 (the lower right panels of Figure 3 and Figure 4). This advantage stems from the difficulty of estimating nuisance functions under low smoothness, which induces large first-stage bias—due to errors in both the nuisance parameter estimates and the initial estimator βinit . Consequently, GOE and LDML fail to achieve √n-consistency (see the lower -right panel). As s increases, all three methods exhibit improvements, with bias, variance, and MSE all decreasing. These empirical findings are consistent with our theoretical results, which indicate that the higher-order estimator enjoys improved statistical properties when the Hölder smoothness of the nuisance parameters is low. Figures 5 and 6 (for Case 1) and Figures 7 and 8 (for Case 2) in Supplementary Material Section S9.1 present normal QQ-plots of our higher-order estimators for β∗ 

> 0

and β∗ 

> 1

across all (n, s )

combinations. These plots support the theoretical conclusion (Theorem 4) that the higher-order estimators are asymptotically normal under appropriate scaling. Finally, we perform sensitivity analyses to examine whether our higher-order estimator is sensitive to the choice of k or to the initial estimator βinit . Recall that, for each continuous covariate, we use 29 a degree-1 B-spline basis with ⌈n/ 100 ⌉ interior knots. To examine the sensitivity to the choice of k,we multiply n/ 100 by a factor λ ∈ { 0.75 , 1, 1.25 }, so that the number of knots varies over ⌈λn/ 100 ⌉,resulting in k = d · (⌈λn/ 100 ⌉ + 1) + 1 . Figure 9 in Supplementary Material Section S9.1 reports the empirical bias, variance, and mean squared error for HOE QTE estimator across different values of λ for both Cases 1 and 2 with n = 2000 and s = 0 .25 , based on 1000 Monte Carlo replications. The figure shows the expected bias–variance tradeoff: as k increases, the empirical bias of the higher-order estimator decreases, whereas the empirical variance increases. In practice, one may choose k such that the point estimates do not differ as much compared to the increase in variance. Here, for both Cases 1 and 2, ⌈n/ 100 ⌉ is a reasonable choice. A more thorough study of how to choose k is left to a future work that is dedicated to the problem of constructing data-driven basis. To assess sensitivity to the initial estimator βinit , we perturb it deterministically as β(δ) 

> init

=

βinit + δ · (−1, 1) ⊤, with δ ∈ {− 0.5, −0.25 , 0, 0.25 , 0.5}. Here, the perturbation level δ controls both the magnitude and the direction of the perturbation. Figure 10 in Supplementary Material Section S9.1 reports boxplots of the estimation error of the HOE QTE estimator across different values of δ for both Cases 1 and 2, in the setting n = 2000 and s = 0 .25 , based on 1000 Monte Carlo replications. The results reported in Figure 10 in Supplementary Material Section S9.1 suggest that HOE is fairly robust to moderate perturbations in the initial estimator. However, when the perturbation becomes too large, the estimator becomes less stable, as reflected by the increased dispersion and a slightly greater number of outliers.

5.2 Real data analysis 

In this section, we apply our higher-order estimators to estimate the QTE of 401(k) plan eligibility on household wealth, using the Survey of Income and Program Participation (SIPP) data studied in Benjamin (2003); Abadie (2003); Chernozhukov and Hansen (2004); Kallus et al. (2024). The sample contains 9,915 observations. The treatment T indicates eligibility for a 401(k) plan, and the outcome Y is net financial assets; our goal is to assess whether 401(k) eligibility increases household saving. The dataset consists of a set of pre-treatment covariates X, including age, income, family size, education, marital status, two-earner status, DB pension status, IRA participation, and homeownership (see Chernozhukov and Hansen (2004), Section 3, for details). Recently, Kallus et al. (2024) analyzed this dataset using LDML estimators. We implement our higher-order debiased estimators on the same dataset to provide a proof-of-concept for their application in real-world settings. As in our simulation studies, we split the sample into two folds. With the nuisance sample, we compute the initial estimator ̂ βinit by stabilized weighting, and estimate both the stabilized weights ̂

ξ and the generalized outcome regression model ̂ bβinit using four learners: random forest, boosting, LASSO, and a one-hidden-layer neural network. Random forests are implemented using the R package randomForest , boosting using gbm , LASSO using hdm , and the one-hidden-layer neural network using nnet . For LASSO, we include polynomial terms of degree 6 for age, 8 for income, 4 for education, and 2 for family size, together with all binary covariates, and all pairwise interactions among these terms, resulting in a total of 275 predictors. In the debiasing step, we use degree-2 B-spline expansions for the continuous covariates income, age, family size, and education, with interior knot numbers (⌈n/ 200 ⌉, ⌈n/ 200 ⌉, 4, 2) = (25 , 25 , 4, 2) ,31 respectively. We then construct ¯zk(x) by stacking the resulting univariate spline basis blocks with the binary covariates and an intercept term. This choice accounts for the heterogeneous supports of the continuous covariates: income and age vary over wide ranges, while family size and education take values on smaller grids. It also ensures numerical stability of ̂ Σk by avoiding very small eigenvalues. For comparison, we also report LDML estimates under the same settings as Section 6.2 of Kallus et al. (2024) (with K = 5 ), using a fixed two-fold split without re-randomizing folds to maintain transparency. 

τ Forest Neural Net Boosting LASSO 0.25 LDML 1.06 1.02 1.04 1.06 HOE 1.01 1.09 1.09 1.11 0.50 LDML 4.95 4.46 4.45 4.75 HOE 4.74 4.37 4.40 4.35 0.75 LDML 13.60 11.90 12.16 12.65 HOE 12.68 11.77 12.15 11.90 Table 2: The QTE of 401(k) eligibility in thousand dollars estimated by LDML and higher-order estimates (HOE) using different regression methods (LASSO, neural network, boosting, and random forests). Here τ ∈ { 25% , 50% , 75% } denotes the quantile level of QTE. Table 2 reports the point estimates of 25%, 50%, and 75% QTEs. Overall, QTE estimates are generally stable among different nuisance learners, suggesting that the bias of the LDML estimator may be relatively small in this application. The higher-order estimates are close to their LDML counterparts, further strengthening this conclusion. Consequently, the difference between the higher-order estimator and the LDML estimator provides empirical evidence on the magnitude of the potential bias of the LDML estimator (Liu et al., 2024). We examine sensitivity to both the choice of k and the initial estimator βinit . To assess sensitivity to k, we increase the number of interior knots for age by multiplying n/ 200 by a factor 

λ ∈ { 0.75 , 1, 1.25 } while keeping other knot numbers fixed. The higher-order estimators perform reasonably well in this range, as shown in Table 3 in Supplementary Material Section S9.2. Stability is ensured by the numerical inversion of ̂ Σk when k is relatively small compared to n, whereas the performance degrades if ̂ Σk approaches singularity. Therefore, a heuristic strategy to explore in the future is to increase k (via knots or degree) until numerical instability occurs. Developing such data-driven rules for selecting k and constructing ¯zk remains an important direction. Sensitivity to βinit is examined similarly to the simulation studies. We find that the higher-order estimator is generally robust to small perturbations of ̂ βinit , as shown in Table 4 in Supplementary Material Section S9.2. 

# 6 Concluding Remarks 

In this paper, we have constructed higher-order estimators for implicitly defined parameters frequently encountered in econometrics, including average treatment effects and quantile treatment effects as special cases. By applying the HOIFs framework to implicitly defined parameters, we have contributed to the existing econometrics and causal inference literature. Our proposed estimators 32 achieve improved convergence rates compared to first-order methods, which requires only that the initial estimator be consistent rather than imposing stringent rate conditions. For binary-treatment QTE, our estimator attains √n-consistency under the Hölder smoothness condition s/d > 1/4,substantially relaxing the requirements of existing approaches and matching the minimal Hölder smoothness condition for the ATE to be √n-estimable. We have also extended the framework to infinite-dimensional GTMs, providing a unified approach for estimating nonparametric causal functions such as dose–response curves. Several directions remain for future research. First, while we focused on second-order estimators assuming that Σk can be estimated sufficiently accurately, relaxing this assumption would require further advances in the theory of higher-order U -processes. Second, extending our framework to other complexity-reducing assumptions, such as sparsity classes (Athey et al., 2018), remains an open question. Third, with slight modifications, our framework is also expected to be able to address endogeneity problems (including nonparametric and many weak IV models) (Breunig and Chen, 2024). Finally, it would be interesting to compare our higher-order debiased estimator with other related bias-correction or bias-aware methods (Armstrong et al., 2020; Zheng et al., 2025; Bonhomme et al., 2026).

# S1 Comparisons of General Treatment Models and Marginal Structural Models 

In this section, we further discuss the similarities and differences between the General Treatment Models (GTM) (Ai et al., 2021) and the Marginal Structural Models (MSM) (See Example 6), a well-known class of semiparametric causal models developed by Robins and colleagues (Robins et al., 2000). MSM was developed, in part, to overcome the curse-of-dimensionality when drawing causal conclusions from interventional or observational studies when the treatment regimes under study are complex. In its simplest form, MSM directly models the counterfactual mean EY (t) as a parameterized function g(t; β∗) of t ∈ T with β∗ := ( β∗ 

> 0

EY (t) = g(t; β∗) = β∗

t. (S1.1) This model is nonparametric because the model imposed on EY (t) does not induce any restrictions on the observed data distribution. When t is a continuous variable, however, finite-dimensional parametric MSM is often specified. For example, the statistician could insist on modeling EY (t) by linear model (S1.1) and believe that the model is the truth. Under unconfoundedness, linear MSM (S1.1) imposes the following restriction on the observed data distribution: 

EY (t) = E[E(Y |X, T = t)] = E[ξ(X, T )Y |T = t] = β∗

E [ξ(X, T ) ( Y − g(T ; β∗)) ω(T )] ≡ 0, ∀ ω : T → R, (S1.3) so induces a conditional moment constraint in the sense of Ai and Chen (2003), leading to a non-trivial orthocomplement to the model tangent space. We also refer readers to Section 3 of Chen and Xie (2025) for a related discussion. In this section, we demonstrate that Assumptions 7(i) and 7(iii) hold for both ATE and QTE from Examples 1 and 2. We first recall the following definition from the empirical process theory (van der Vaart and Wellner, 2023). 

Definition 2 (VC-subgraph class) . A collection F of measurable functions is called a VC-subgraph class, or a VC-class, if the collection of all subgraphs of the functions (see Section 2.6.2 of van der Vaart and Wellner (2023)) in F forms a VC-class of sets (see Section 2.6.1 of van der Vaart and Wellner (2023)). N (FATE , ∥ · ∥ Q, 2, ϵ ∥FATE ∥Q, 2) ≤ C (16 e) ϵ−2, 0 < ϵ < 1,

# S5 On the Relationship Between Higher-Order Estimators and Other Related Proposals in Econometrics 

As suggested by a referee, in this section, we illustrate the connection between the higher-order estimators studied in this paper and several other bias correction methods in the recent econometrics literature, including Cattaneo et al. (2019), Cattaneo and Jansson (2018), and Cattaneo et al. (2025). 

S5.1 Discussion of Cattaneo et al. (2019) 

Cattaneo et al. (2019) study two-step semiparametric estimation and inference in settings where the first-step linear regression may include many covariates. To keep the exposition simple, we focus on the ATE case and take the target parameter to be β∗ = E[Y (1)] . To facilitate comparison with our construction, we characterize β∗ via the moment condition 

ψ(β∗) = E [ξ(X, T ){Y − b(X, T = 1) } + b(X, T = 1) − β∗] = 0 , (S5.5) where ξ(x, t ) = t/ P(T = 1 |X = x) and b(x, t ) = tE[Y |X = x, T = 1] are nuisance functions. 8In this example, we first would like to emphasize that Cattaneo et al. (2019) and our paper target different parameters, which will be discussed in detail shortly after. However, Cattaneo et al. (2019) and our paper share a common feature, which is that both consider to use linear models (of a possibly nonlinear k-dimensional feature map/basis ¯ϕk of (X, T )) to approximate certain quantities related to nuisance parameters, such that the target parameters of Cattaneo et al. (2019) and ours both reduce to bilinear functionals, with their precise forms to be revealed thereafter. In particular, Cattaneo et al. (2019) use linear models to directly approximate the nuisance parameters, whereas we use a linear approximation to represent the nuisance estimation errors ; when the nuisance estimates are zeros, these two approaches collapse. For such bilinear functionals, a naïve plug-in estimator can have non-negligible leave-in bias in high-dimensional settings when the dimension 

k ≳ √n (Cattaneo et al., 2019). In terms of bias correction strategies, Cattaneo et al. (2019) start from the plug-in estimator and apply a jackknife-based correction, whereas we exploit the bilinear structure directly and construct an unbiased U -statistic estimator. We show below that, for bilinear functionals, these two strategies yield the same estimator. To facilitate the analysis below, let ¯zk(X) ∈ Rk be a dictionary for X. Since T is binary, define 

¯ϕk(X, T ) = T ¯zk(X) and the k × k population Gram matrix Σk := E[ ¯ϕk(X, T )⊗2] = E[T ¯zk(X)⊗2].Strictly following Cattaneo et al. (2019), they are interested in solving for β∗ of the estimating equation ψ(β∗) = 0 in (S5.5) , and postulate linear approximations for the nuisance components ξ

and b. Specifically, we assume that 

ξ(Xi, T i) = ¯ϕk(Xi, T i)⊤γξ + e1i, E[ ¯ϕk(Xi, T i)e1i] = 0 ,b(Xi, T i) = ¯ϕk(Xi, T i)⊤γb + e2i, E[ ¯ϕk(Xi, T i)e2i] = 0 ,

where e1i and e2i are the best linear approximation error, and (γξ, γ b) are the population least-squares coefficients, i.e., 

γξ = Σ −1 

> k

E[ξ(X, T )¯ zk(X)] = Σ −1 

> k

E[¯ zk(X)]

> k

E[b(X, T )¯ zk(X)] = Σ −1 

> k

E[T Y ¯zk(X)] .

> n

E{̂ ψ(1) (β)} − ψ(β) = E[{̂ ξ(X, T ) − ξ(X, T )}{ b(X, T ) −̂ b(X, T )}]. (S5.6) 9Consistent with the main text, we employ sample splitting: the nuisance estimators ̂ ξ and ̂ b are obtained from an independent nuisance sample and all expectations E[·] heretofore in this section are taken with respect to the main sample conditional on the nuisance sample. Hence ̂ ξ and ̂ b can be treated as fixed functions. Our framework imposes no linearity restriction on ξ and b. We allow ̂ ξ and ̂ b to be obtained using flexible methods (e.g., kernel smoothing, series estimation, or machine learning), although to obtain sharp theoretical results, we sometimes impose certain smoothness assumptions on ξ −̂ ξ and 

b −̂ b such that the truncation bias that cannot be captured by the basis ¯ϕk is sufficiently small. We project the nuisance approximation residuals ̂ ξ − ξ and ̂ b − b onto the sieve space spanned by 

¯ϕk(x, t ). As described in Section 3.1, this projection leads to a decomposition of (S5.6) into (i) a truncation bias TB ψ,k and (ii) a part of the bias (S5.6) captured by the k-dimensional dictionary 

¯ϕk. The latter can be written as a bilinear functional

Lemma 6 in the end of this subsection shows that the jackknife bias-corrected estimator coincides exactly with our second-order U -statistic estimator, i.e., ̂
