The above derivation shows that, in the special case where the target functional admits the bilinear representation Bψ,k = α⊤ 

> k

Σ−1 

> k

ηk, the two constructions ̂ Bψ,k and ̂ Bψ,k, jackknife yield the same 

U -statistic. The key ingredient of our approach is to first construct a representation like Bψ,k and estimate Bψ,k by a natural U -statistic. By contrast, the jackknife-based bias correction method of Cattaneo et al. (2019) can be viewed as a general bias correction scheme for parameters such as Bψ,k ,for which standard plug-in estimators (such as the standard M -estimation) have non-negligible bias when the dimension is large relative to √n. It is worth mentioning that the approach in Cattaneo et al. (2019) can deal with parameters of a more generic nonlinear form than the bilinear form Bψ,k ,but the theoretical results are restricted to k = O(√n) due to the nonlinearity of the more general problems considered. For Bψ,k , we have seen that the U -statistic estimator is unbiased for any k

when Σk is known. Finally, if Σk is unknown and is replaced by ̂ Σk, our Supplementary Material Section S3 derives HOIFs of Bψ,k to use higher-order U -statistics for further bias correction. To our knowledge, systematically extending jackknife-based corrections to control such higher-order remainder terms seems to be beyond the scope of Cattaneo et al. (2019), but we suspect that constructions similar to those in HOIFs can be considered. We also refer interested readers to Lin et al. (2024) for a recent result in this direction. Finally, we record Lemma 6 and its proof below.

S5.2 Discussion of Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) 

We next discuss how the bootstrap-based bias correction approach developed in Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) is also connected to our higher-order estimators. Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) study two-step semiparametric estimation and inference with kernel-based first-step estimators, allowing the first step to converge slowly when the bandwidth is small. To facilitate comparison with our framework, we again use the ATE example in Section S5.2, where the target moment ψ(β) is linear in the nuisance functions ξ and b. In this linear case, the bias analyzed in Cattaneo and Jansson (2018) is mainly driven by the leave-in bias that results from the use of the same sample in both steps, when the bandwidth is small. They show that, certain nonparametric bootstrap procedures can also recover the leading bias term and hence deliver valid inference. Our construction allows the nuisance estimators ̂ ξ and ̂ b to be obtained by generic learners, such as random forests, or neural networks. By using sample splitting, we focus on the approximation 12 bias. We first approximate this bias by a bilinear functional Bψ,k induced by a dictionary-based projection, and then estimate this functional directly via a U -statistic estimator. Despite this difference, the two approaches are closely connected. As discussed in Section S5.2, once the target is reduced to the bilinear functional Bψ,k , we will show that the resulting bootstrap bias-corrected estimator is asymptotically equivalent to our U -statistics-based estimator ̂ Bψ,k .Let {Oi = ( Yi, T i, X i)}ni=1 be the original sample and let O∗

In this section, we establish refined local maximal inequalities for U -processes that can be used to establish convergence rates and asymptotic normality of higher-order estimators considered in this paper. These are key technical tools for the main results in Sections 3.3 and 4.2, and may also be of independent interest for other related applications. We begin by introducing the necessary notation. 

S6.1 Notation and Review of Concepts from U -Process Theory 

We first recall the key notation introduced in Section 1.3, and then introduce additional terminologies for U -statistics and U -processes that will be used in the technical lemmas that follow. Recall that Un,m is the m-th order U -statistic operator, i.e., 

Un,m h ≡ Un,m h(O1, · · · , O m) = (n − m)!

> 1≤i1̸=···̸ =im≤n

for any measurable function (kernel) h : Om → R. So, Un, 1 ≡ Pn is understood to be the empirical mean operator. Let Sm denote the symmetrization of a function of m variables, 

Smh(O1, · · · , O m) = 1

m!

∑ 

> 1≤i1̸=···̸ =im≤m

h(Oi1 , · · · , O im ).

Because a U -statistic averages over unordered subsets, Un,m h = Un,m 

(Smh). Accordingly, all subsequent notation and results are stated for symmetric kernels; when a kernel is asymmetric, they apply by default to its symmetrized version. For any symmetric measurable function h : Om → R and j = 1 , . . . , m , let Pm−j h denote the function on Oj defined by 

Pm−j h(o1, ..., o j ) = E[h(o1, . . . , o j , O j+1 , . . . , O m)] =

P0h ≡ h and Pmh ≡ E[h(O1, . . . , O m)] . We also introduce the following shorthand notation for Hoeffding projection of a symmetric measurable function h, for j ≤ m:

πj h(o1, . . . , o j ) := ( δo1 − P) · · · (δoj − P)Pm−j h(o1, . . . , o m). (S6.2) For a non-symmetric kernel h we work with its symmetrised form Smh; all the above definitions are understood with that replacement, i.e., Pm−j h ≡ Pm−j Smh and πj h ≡ πj Smh. We next review some basic terminologies about U -processes used throughout. Let F be a class of symmetric measurable functions f : Om → R. We assume that there is a symmetric measurable envelope F for F, that is sup f ∈F |f | ≤ F, such that PmF2 < ∞. We will assume certain uniform 16 covering number conditions for the function class F. Let ∥ · ∥ F denote the supremum norm over 

F, i.e. ∥f ∥F := sup f ∈F |f |. For a probability measure Q and a constant p > 1, let ∥ · ∥ Q,p denote the Lp(Q)-seminorm, i.e., ∥f ∥Q,p := {∫ |f (x)|pdQ(x)}1/p for finite q while ∥f ∥Q, ∞ denotes the essential supremum of |f | with respect to Q. For any function class F with ∥F∥Q,p > 0, we use 

N(F, ∥ · ∥ Q,p , ϵ ∥F∥Q,p ) to denote the minimal number of ∥ · ∥ Q,p -balls of radius ϵ∥F∥Q,p needed to cover F. See Section 2.1 of van der Vaart and Wellner (2023) for details. For j = 1 , . . . , m , we define the uniform entropy integral 

Jj (δ, F, F) :=  

> ∫δ
> 0

> Q
> {

1 + log N(Pm−j F, ∥ · ∥ Q, 2, τ ∥Pm−j F∥Q, 2)

> }j/ 2

dτ, 

where Pm−j F = {Pm−j f : f ∈ F} and sup Q is taken over all finitely discrete distributions on Oj . It is straightforward to verify that Pm−j F is an envelope for Pm−j F. To avoid measurability difficulties, we will assume that F is pointwise measurable. If F is pointwise measurable and PmF < ∞ (which we have assumed), then πj F := {πj f : f ∈ F} and Pm−j F for j = 1 , . . . , m are all pointwise measurable by the dominated convergence theorem. For completeness, we recall Definitions 1 and 2. 

Definition 1 (VC-subgraph class) . A collection F of measurable functions is called a VC-subgraph class, or a VC-class, if the collection of all subgraphs of the functions (see Section 2.6.2 of van der Vaart and Wellner (2023)) in F forms a VC-class of sets (see Section 2.6.1 of van der Vaart and Wellner (2023)). 

Definition 2 (VC type class) . A function class F with envelope F is said to be of VC type with characteristic (A, v ) if sup Q N (F, ∥ · ∥ Q, 2, ϵ ∥F∥Q, 2) ≤ (A/ϵ )v for all ϵ ∈ (0 , 1] , where sup Q denotes the supremum over all finitely discrete probability measures. 

S6.2 Local Maximal Inequalities for U -Processes 

In this section we allow the function class to depend explicitly on the sample size n. Throughout, we denote a generic function class by Fn to emphasize this dependence and derive non-asymptotic bounds that hold for each fixed n.Chen and Kato (2020) establish local maximal inequalities for U -processes indexed by a VC-type class Fn := {f : O2 → R}, whose envelope is denoted by Fn. Their bounds are expressed in terms of the VC characteristics (An, v n) and quantities including sup f ∈Fn ∥P2−kf ∥Pk ,2 and ∥P2−kFn∥Pk ,2

for k = 1 , 2 (see their Corollary 5.5). Because their bound uses the projected envelope PFn(·)

(i.e. the conditional mean of Fn in one of the arguments), it can be unnecessarily loose when PFn

is of greater magnitude in order than the quantity of actual interest, sup f ∈Fn |Pf |. To sharpen the analysis, we work directly with sup f ∈Fn |Pf | and derive a refined maximal inequality tailored specifically for our problem. The next lemma records this result; as indicated, it is particularly useful when sup f ∈Fn |Pf | is much less than PFn.

Remark 14. For the class Fn = H2,n defined in (S7.8) in Section S7.1, Fn is of VC type with fixed characteristics (4 A, v ) and envelope Fn = H 2,n . In this case, the following hold: ∥PFn∥P,2 ≤∥Fn∥P2,2 ≲ √k, ∥Fn(·)∥∞ ≲ k, sup f ∈Fn ∥f ∥P2,2 ≲ √k with k ≲ n2. Moreover, because each Pf (·)

is a conditional expectation (recall from the notation (S6.1) ), (S7.1) implies sup f ∈Fn ∥Pf (·)∥∞ ≲ 1

and sup f ∈Fn ∥Pf ∥P,2 ≲ 1. Plugging these bounds into Corollary 5.5 of Chen and Kato (2020) renders all higher–order projection terms negligible, leaving the first order term:

√n .

Instead, our maximal inequality replaces the projected envelope PFn by the tighter quantity 

sup f ∈Fn |Pf |, and PFn only influences the bound through the VC characteristics. Thus, PFn only appears in a logarithmic factor, which in turn is dominated by log n. Specifically, Lemma 9 (ii) implies that:

Even when n ≲ k ≲ n2, the right–hand side of the above display still shrinks to zero. Hence, the refined inequality remains informative in the regime where the bound by directly applying results in Chen and Kato (2020) is not sufficiently sharp. ■

# S7 Proof of Results in Section 3.3 

Before starting the proof, we first summarize some frequently used results. Under Assumptions 6(iii) and 8(i)–(iii), Lemma 16 of Liu et al. (2017) implies the following uniform bound: for any function 

f ∈ C(X × T),

β out of subscripts into argument parentheses. For notational simplicity, hereafter we sometimes write Γβ := Γ( ·, ·, β) and denote ̂ Ξβ := ̂ Ξ( O1, O 2; β) if this causes no ambiguity.

Remark 16. In Assumptions 3 and 7, we have assumed that the observations are bounded. For the CLT established in Lemma 16, the boundedness assumption can be relaxed if one uses Corollary 1.4 in Bhattacharya and Ghosh (1992) instead of their Corollary 1.5. The former only requires certain moment conditions to be satisfied, avoiding making the stronger boundedness assumption. We decide to stick to the boundedness assumption, following the related literature on HOIFs (Liu et al., 2017; Robins et al., 2023). ■

In this section, we collect supplementary information on the simulation study conducted in Section 5.1. and Figure 6 for β∗

below for Case 1 and Figure 7 for β∗

for Case 2. It is quite clear that the higher-order estimator proposed here for QTE with binary treatment is asymptotically normally distributed across different sample sizes 

n ∈ { 250 , 500 , 750 , 1000 , 1500 , 2000 , 2500 , 3000 }

and different smoothness levels of the nuisance parameters with s = 0 .25 , 0.4, 0.6.The GOE estimator (Ai et al., 2021) is obtained by solving (6) with ̂ ξ estimated through the following quadratic programming problem: 

min 

> ξ
> n

> i=1

Here, ξ = ( ξ1, . . . , ξ n) with ξi ≡ ξ(Xi, T i), and ¯ϕk is a power series basis of length k. The parameter 

k is selected by minimizing a cross-validation criterion.
